## Page 62

Behind Man Ray’s Seminal Photograph of  Kiki, “Queen of Montparnasse” 
Art & Photography - The Story Behind The Image 
AnOther reveals the story behind Man Ray’s iconic Noir et Blanche, one of the most important examples ever of Surrealist 
photography 
This month marks the release of a trilogy of photo books by Taschen, each dedicated to an American 20th-century photographic 
“master of light”,  Man Ray, Paul Outerbridge and Edward Weston. Here, in the first of a three-part dedicated series of features, we 
explore the story behind an iconic pair of images from the first, Noir et Blanche by Man Ray, “the multi-talented master of Modernist 
imagery”. 
When young American painter and photographer Emmanuel Radnitzky, better known as Man Ray, followed his friend Marcel 
Duchamp from New York to Paris in 1921, he finally found himself among likeminded souls. The experimental pair, who met in 
Ridgefield, New Jersey in 1915, had been busy trying to pioneer Dadaism in the Big Apple, co-editing the magazine New York Dada, 
which turned out to be an epic failure. Thus followed their move to Paris, where the rest of the Dada group were residing, and 
Man Ray’s life-changing introduction to André Breton, Jacques Rigaud et al on July 22, 1921.  
Breton saw in Man Ray a useful accomplice, employing his photographic skills to capture artworks for inclusion in Breton’s 
magazine  Littérature in return for publishing Man Ray’s portraiture. Through this, and word-of-mouth recommendation, Man Ray 
had soon been introduced to all of Paris’s key players, from Gertrude Stein, Picasso and Jean Cocteau to the many wealthy 
aristocrats keen for a portraitist. Eventually he came to the attention of couturier Paul Poiret, and by 1924 was regularly 
photographing Parisian haute couture for Vogue. 
Man Ray never approached photography in a traditional manner. For him – a self-proclaimed painter, first and foremost – it was a 
medium ripe for manipulation. In late 1921, he developed his eponymous “rayograph” technique – the result of accidentally 
switching on the darkroom light mid-development – a process that creates a reverse image of a photograph in which the light and 
dark areas are dramatically switched. His early images using the method are widely credited as the first instances of Surrealist 
photography. Perhaps surprisingly, the fashion world were enamored by this artful doctoring, which they saw as elevating the 
status of photography to that of an art form, and in 1922 Vanity Fair included a four of his rayographs in a feature. Man Ray was 
delighted by such widespread dissemination of his work and not long after proclaimed that he would always “combine art and 
fashion” in his commercial projects. 


## Page 63

As a result, many of his most revered works were in fact taken for fashion magazines, including his seminal 1926 image Noir 
et Blanche, and its reverse negative print, depicting Man Ray’s muse Kiki de Montparnasse, her pale face resting on a table 
and her hand holding up a wooden African mask whose beatific expression she is mimicking. The duo had met in 1921, when 
Man Ray, allured by the actress and singer’s “cute accent and air of mystery”, had asked her to pose for him. The pair lived 
together and shared a 12 year relationship as lovers but eventually separated, although Montparnasse continued to model 
for him on and off for many years. 
This iconic image first appeared in the May 1926 edition of French Vogue, alongside the evocative caption “Mother of Pearl 
Face and Ebony Mask”.  
It is a majestic study in tone and texture: the patches of light on the dark, lustrous mask are echoed by those punctuating 
Montparnasse’s shiny black hair, while, in contrast, her soft, porcelain-hued face and shoulder boast delicate patches of 
shadow. These juxtapositions are masterfully highlighted in the reverse negative, in which the mask and Montparnasse’s hair 
(including her eyelashes and brows and rouged lips radiate light while her skin is the colour of charcoal. These two 
enduringly powerful images – which would go on to inspire Jean-Baptiste Mondino’s 1999 advert for Jean Paul Gaultier's 
signature Classique – embody the words of Emmanuelle de L’Ecotais, who writes in an essay for the Taschen tome, “Man 
Ray wrote with light bulbs just as poets write with pens. White light replaced black ink. Man Ray became the supreme poet, 
writing with light.” 
Daisy Woodward 


## Page 64

Kiki de Montparnasse | Prima Donna Muse - Written by Lauren Dickinson Clarke 
My alluring Prima Donna candle is inspired by the vivacious Kiki De Montparnasse, unifying muse for a generation of Parisian 
Dadaists and Surrealists in the 1920’s. 
It is said that there was something both captivating and unknowable about Kiki, rendering her the perfect and irresistible 
muse. So be prepared because you are heading on a journey of intimidating beauty and a frequently unclothed body... 
ALICE PRIN 
Alice Ernestine Prin came into the world on October 2nd, 1901, in Châtillon-sur-Seine, France. But little did everyone know that 
this young country-girl was later to be known as Kiki De Montparnasse, style icon and artist force in her own right. 
When Kiki was just a small child, her Mother decided to leave for Paris to better her own life and leave poor Kiki behind. 
Instead, Kiki was left in the care of her Grandmother who was not equipped with the tools that she needed to raise a small 
child. Kiki and her Grandmother struggled to make ends meet and found themselves in anguishing poverty. Rats were 
frequent visitors to the house and the stench of rotting food attacked their senses. So, at the age of 12, Kiki decided that she 
would join her Mother in Paris in search of a better life. 
Kiki had bright dreams of her future in Paris but the reality she faced when she arrived was certainly more gloomy than bright. 
In desperate need of money, Kiki took up work in a bakery, but the pay was a mere pittance and so Kiki still struggled to get 
by. Kiki had never been taught about money and as her parents weren’t married when Kiki was born, Kiki didn’t know her 
Father or even, where he was. 
After living in Paris for a short while, her Mother decided to send Kiki to school. However, our rebellious Kiki only lasted in 
education for a year. After all, school was boring, and she had set her sights on something so much bigger! Kiki believed that 
great things awaited her. So, in 1917, Kiki set out to explore the alluring the world of modeling… 
Kiki was excited about her new modeling endeavor. For the first time in her life, she had experienced a little male attention 
and was able to make a small income. Dolefully however, her Mother didn’t agree. 
She thought that Kiki was degrading herself and that modeling was simply a way for artists to leer over young women. These 
clashing views escalated and soon the two were arguing constantly. Before she knew it, Kiki found herself homeless on the 
streets of Paris and facing poverty once more. 


## Page 65

STREET RAT 
Kiki was excited about her new modeling endeavor. For the first time in her life, she had received a little male attention and was 
able to make a small living. Dolefully however, her Mother most certainly didn’t agree. 
She thought that Kiki was degrading herself and that modeling was simply a way for artists to leer over young women. These 
clashing views escalated and soon the two were arguing constantly. Before she knew it, Kiki found herself homeless on the 
streets of Paris and facing poverty once more. 
These newfound circumstances were most certainly a setback. But Kiki was determined to find her fortune one way or 
another… Kiki sought refuge by living with some friends that she'd met during her short time in Paris. They would share their 
food with her and offered her somewhere to sleep. Kiki used her modeling experience to her advantage and set out to find new 
artists who might need a muse. Boy, did she find them. Before she knew it, Kiki was definitely modeling again. Only this time it 
was in the nude! 
NOTORIOUS MONTPARNASSE 
So here Kiki was, a vagrant living on the streets of Paris and stealing a night’s sleep inside wherever she could. She was 
modeling nude in exchange for food & shelter, but vibrant Kiki’s spirits were never dampened. She wore a smile regardless of 
her unforgiving situation and forever exuded fun & joviality. One day, when she was meandering the streets of Paris in search of 
new ‘breaks’, she came across Montparnasse, an area in the south of Paris on the left bank of the river Seine. 
Montparnasse was like nowhere she had ever seen. It erupted with cabarets & bohemian life. The atmosphere was electric. This 
was the epicenter of artists, literature, absinthe & loose living in Paris and Kiki was enthralled! She embraced her newfound 
home with fervor and before long she had made many friends. But not just any friends. For It was here that Kiki forged a 
friendship with a Russian painter named ‘Chaim Soutine’. Chaim Soutine took Kiki under his wing & eventually introduced Kiki to 
other artists in his circle. These included ‘Maurice Utrillo’ who specialized in cityscapes, poet ‘Jean Cocteau’ who was a man of 
many talents, ‘Fujita Tsuguharu’ a Japanese-French painter and ‘Modigliani’, an Italian-Jewish painter and sculptor. 


## Page 66

During the year of 1918, Kiki met a French-Polish painter named ‘Maurice Mendjisky’. Very quickly, Kiki and Maurice’s 
friendship escalated to a passionate a love-affair. Maurice embraced Kiki as his muse and used her as his model for a series of 
portraits. This then opened a window for Kiki to become a muse to nearly all of the leading male avant-garde artists in France 
at that time. Needless to say, Kiki jumped at this chance! It is also said that it was in fact Maurice who gave Kiki her new and 
shorter name, ‘Kiki De Montparnasse’. 
ENTER MAN RAY... 
‘Man Ray’ was a revered American visual artist and photographer who spent the majority of his time in Paris. In a stroke of 
luck which would change Kiki’s life, she and Man Ray crossed paths one sunny day in Paris. Kiki was known for breaking the 
hearts of her artists. She had become used to meaningless love affairs and would discard men like last night’s supper. Little 
did she know that Man Ray was about to steal her heart. 
Kiki (obviously) soon started modeling for Man Ray and the two set about creating memorable & exciting works of art. She 
appeared in his film ‘Le Retour à la raison’ which gave her an insight into the world of acting and film. Kiki quickly warmed to 
the idea of becoming an actress. So, with the support of Man Ray, she traveled to America solo to test out her newfound 
talents. However, this adventure was short lived. Shortly before an audition, Kiki decided that she wanted to go shopping 
and well, managed to miss the whole thing! 
Kiki longed for Paris and her lover Man Ray. Being apart from both of them made her realize how important the City of Love 
was to her… and of course her relationship too. 
Once her feet were firmly on back on the streets of Paris, Kiki rid the thoughts of a film career. Instead, she decided to focus 
her efforts on new creative endeavors such as painting. She continued to model for Man Ray and even appeared in a few 
avant-garde films that he directed.

## Page 67

Have you ever come across a photographic piece of art which features a woman with f-holes on her back? Well, the woman 
in this photograph is Kiki De Montparnasse and the artist behind this striking image is Man Ray! The photograph was taken 
in 1924 and is named ‘Le Violon d’Ingres’. The image was made with the intent of comparing Kiki’s curves to that of a 
musical instrument and was regarded as the most surrealist photograph to be taken yet. 
Le Violin d'Ingres, Man Ray, 1924 
“ The opening drew the neighborhood regulars out in masse. From five o'clock until past midnight, they came in a continuous 
stream and the little gallery vibrated with passionate comments. To our knowledge, it was the most successful opening of the 
year. Those who had come to have fun stayed to buy, and before the night was over, a large number of canvases were adorned 
with the little white cardboard marked "sold."   HERALD TRIBUNE 
Although it was what had initially shaped her success, Kiki wasn’t always just a muse to avant-gardists. In 1927, Kiki had her 
very own successful exhibition. All of her works were displayed at the Sacre du Printemps gallery, and they had almost 
entirely sold out by the end! Two years after her exhibition, she decided to publish her memoirs which would lift the veil on 
the roaring 1920’s in Montparnasse. The introduction to Kiki’s raw & scandalous memoirs was written by the brilliant Ernest 
Hemingway. Published in France and translated into English too, it became quite the popular read. 
Readers in America however, was a little less keen to embrace Kiki’s pennings. Rumour has it that some of the stories 
proved to be a little too risqué for their wholesome American taste! 
By the end of the 1920’s Kiki’s many talents span across modelling, painting, writing and acting… What more could she 
possibly do? Well, throughout the 1930’s/1940’s, Kiki honed her singing talents and even made some recordings too. 
A few of Kiki’s songs: 
‘La Haut Sur La Butte’ 
‘Les Marins de Groix’ 
‘La Volerie’ 
‘Kiki Etait Belle’ 


## Page 68

THE LAST DANCE 
Kiki, May 3, 1929 at the charity ball of the friendly help of artists
Kiki’s colorful life was alight!  She was a respected muse & had finally made her small fortune by exploiting her various 
talents. Sadly though, and as in all great stories, a turn for the worst was about to come. Her art & party filled lifestyle soon 
extended its hand of temptation and Kiki found herself immersed in alcohol & drugs. To make it all the worse, the 
excitement around Kiki’s work soon began to fade and before long, Kiki was singing for the tourists of Paris in order to fund 
her violent addictions. It wasn’t long at all before Kiki’s health started to deteriorate and she soon became dreadfully ill. 
Although she tried many times to put the bad habits to a halt, she couldn’t help but eventually give in to temptation. 
Despite her demons, Kiki continued to play the role of ‘muse’ and was sought after by many artists. But in a turbulent twist, 
in 1939, Kiki was arrested for the abuse of cocaine which led to her being institutionalized. After her release, Kiki vowed to 
quit her damaging habit. Woefully, her attempt was futile as poor Kiki was arrested for the same offence in 1946. Following 
her second release, Kiki continued in her addictive battle and was eventually seen collapsing in front of her apartment. 
Due to medical complications associated with her continued use of alcohol & drugs,  Kiki tragically passed away. Many 
artists & fans gathered to attend her funeral, and even followed to see her burial in Montparnasse Cemetery. Kiki was 
buried in the place that she loved most amongst the people who had supported her passions. 
Her tombstone identified her as Kiki, ‘The Queen of Montparnasse’. 
Kiki was known for her creativity, frankness & bravery. She rose from poverty stricken beginnings to inspire artists who 
thanked her, produced astonishing works. Kiki was unashamedly fearless in her approach to life and proved that with grit & 
persistence, the boldest dreams can be realized, even when you start with nothing.

## Page 69

Man Ray, Kiki With African Mask 
Zuzanna Stańska 23 July 2017 
Man Ray, above - Kiki with African Mask, 1924, National Gallery of Victoria, Melbourne, copyrights, Man Ray Trust/ADAGP, Paris 
His name was Emmanuel Radnitsky, her name was Alice Prin, but we know them as Man Ray and Kiki. They were the heroes of 
Montparnasse in the Twenties. He was a painter and a photographer; she was his model and also his lover for a while. 
Kiki of Montparnasse was born in 1901 in Burgundy as a child to unmarried, poor woman. She was a classic beauty with jet-
black hair cut short and curly. She was a model and agreed to pose for Man Ray, who she thought had “a cute accent and an 
air of mystery.” In 1921 they were living together, first at the tiny Hôtel Istria. Their torrid love affair has been documented 
often. Kiki became as famous in what she called “the land of liberty.” 


## Page 70

But although she and Man Ray had become famous, their relationship faded. She continued to be the 
central character and most famous personality of the bistro set in Montparnasse, while every 
American artist visited Man Ray’s studios. He invented the “rayograph,” while Kiki left for America, 
where she was going to follow a career in the movies–it turned out to be an utter failure.  She 
eventually returned to Paris and began to paint. She even had a very successful exhibition in March 
1927. 
But before it happened, in May 1926 French Vogue published a photograph by Man Ray accompanied 
by the elegant description ‘Mother of Pearl Face and Ebony Mask’. The woman is of course Kiki. As its 
first published title suggests, the work is concerned with the contrasts and associations created by 
juxtapositions: human body and inanimate object; white and black; the modern world and traditional 
life; France and Africa. 
It’s one of the symbols of art deco; now in the National Gallery of Victoria collection.

Her maquillage was a work of art in itself ...her mouth painted a deep scarlet that

angle, but I liked it best in full profile, when it had the lineal purity of a stuffed salmon.

## Page 82

Montparnasse Neighborhood
The Montparnasse district in Paris was a cultural hub in the 1920s, known for its 
artistic and intellectual life, and home to many notable artists and writers including 
Picasso, Hemingway, Modigliani, Chagall, Soutine & Fujita to name a few.

## Page 84

Kiki's Hangouts 
Still Thriving 100 Years Later

## Page 87

Alice Prin is credited as the author of Kiki de Montparnasse. Prin was a French model, painter, chanteuse, and memoirist during the 
Jazz Age. She was nicknamed the "Queen of Montparnasse" and was a central figure in the liberated culture of Paris in the 1920s. 
Prin's memoirs were first published in English in 1930 but were banned in the United States until the 1970s due to their explicit content. 
In the 1950s and 1960s, the book was reprinted under the title The Education of a Young Model. 
There is also a book called Kiki de Montparnasse by Jose-Luis Bocquet and Catel Muller. Bocquet is a writer and former journalist, and 
Muller is an author of children's books and graphic novels.  Kiki's Memoirs is a 1929 autobiography by Alice Prin (October 2, 1901 – April 
29, 1953), known as Kiki de Montparnasse; a model, artist, and actress working in Montparnasse, Paris in the first half of the twentieth 
century. 
Translated from French to English by Samuel Putnam, and published in Manhattan by Black Manikin Press, her memoirs are a lively 
account of the bohemian lifestyle typical among artists in Paris during the 1920s, with an introduction provided by Ernest Hemingway. 
She tells of her encounters with Amedeo Modigliani, Man Ray, Tsuguharu Foujita, Moïse Kisling, Jean Cocteau, Kees van Dongen, 
Chaïm Soutine, and others. The memoirs were first published in English in 1930, but due to their sometimes explicit content, were 
banned in the United States until the 1970s. 
However, the book had been reprinted under the title The Education of a Young Model throughout the 1950s and 1960s, e.g. a 1954 
edition by Bridgehead has the Hemingway Introduction, photos and illustrations by Mahlon Blaine. These editions were mainly put out 
by Samuel Roth. Taking advantage of the fact that the banning of the book meant it did not receive copyright protection in the U.S., 
Roth put out a series of supposedly copyrighted editions, which were never registered with the Library of Congress, which altered the 
text and added illustrations – line drawings and photographs – which were not by Prin. Editions published in and after 1955 include an 
extra 10 chapters supposedly written by Prin 23 years after the original book, including a visit to New York where she meets again with 
Samuel Roth and Ernest Hemingway; none of this was true. A copy of the first US edition (1930) was held in the section for banned 
books in the New York Public Library through the 1970s. A new edition, edited by Billy Klüver and Julie Martin, was released in 1996.[1] 
 Kiki, the  Author and Memoirist

doorway on left. Rosalies was frequented by, among other French masters, Picasso, Modigliani "Kiki de 
Montparnasse" and Leah Hirsig supported herself by working long  grueling shifts here in the early spring 
of 1925. Kiki's given name was Alice Earnestine Prin Her legacy/namesake became the inspiration for a

## Page 95

World famous 1906 Restaurant - Chez Rosalie - Press
“Rapture for Cooking” 
Excerpt - Rachel Hope Cleves 
Jimmie the Barman, who poured the drinks at many of the best-known Lost Generation drinking holes including the Dingo, the 
Falstaff, and the Trois et As, once observed that it was “remarkable that the leaders and organizers of Montparnasse were largely 
women.” 
Poets like Mina Loy, artists like Nina Hamnett, writers like Djuna Barnes, editors like Sylvia Beach, socialites like Nancy Cunard, 
Artist Muses like "Kiki de Montparnasse" who in 1928 would be crowned Kiki, "Queen of Montparnasse".  Party girls like Flossie 
Martin, crowded the café terraces of Montparnasse in the 1920s. In previous generations, working-class girls and working girls 
had flocked to the city’s nightlife. But after World War I, as never before, bohemian women declared their independence from 
traditional social conventions by downing drinks, smoking cigarettes, romancing lovers, and making art in public view. 
They also enjoyed taking meals in public, at the city’s many inexpensive restaurants. Elsewhere I've written about the voracious 
appetites that Gwen Le Gallienne, Yvette Ledoux, and Caridad de Laberdesque, displayed during multi-course meals at Chez 
Rosalie.  For decades run by "Mere Rosalie Tobia" a former Italian model of Modigliani.  Salto’s, another Italian restaurant was 
also known for its massive portions. Eating at restaurants symbolized the liberated woman’s freedom from the prison of the 
kitchen. They exchanged pots and pans, the instruments of repression, for the pen and paintbrush. 
(The blog kitchen flânerie discusses feminism, cooking, and the 1975 film “Semiotics of the Kitchen” But for other bohemian 
women, the kitchen, far from a prison, could be a place for taking pleasure. These women felt, in Kay Boyle’s words, a “rapture 
for cooking,” which they indulged with the same greediness that they took lovers. In 1968, Boyle, a modernist writer best known 
for her short stories, edited and reprinted Being Geniuses Together, her friend Robert McAlmon’s memoir of 1920s Paris, which 
she intermixed with alternating chapters recalling her own experiences of the era. 
An Intimate History of Good Food and Wicked Sex - Rachel Hope Cleves 
Alice B. Toklas, cooking, feminism, Gertrude Stein, James Joyce, John Glassco, Kay Boyle, Le Havre, Lost Generation, Montparnasse, 
Paris, Raymond Duncan, Robert McAlmon, Trianon 
“My Generation Doesn’t Eat Supper” 
Excerpt - Rachel Hope Cleves 
The young North American men who flocked to Montparnasse in the 1920s came to drink not to eat. The women were 
another matter.  The men of the “Lost Generation,” as they were famously dubbed by Gertrude Stein, scorned the previous 
generations of wealthy tourists, like Henry James’s Christopher Newman, who came to improve their “taste” and often started 
their educations in Paris’s restaurants. 
“My generation doesn’t eat supper,” author and editor Robert McAlmon once shouted at the wife of Canadian author Morley 
Callaghan when she suggested their party of drinkers find a good restaurant. “I’m having another drink. Waiter, five whiskies and 
water!” Or so John Glassco, another young Canadian author at the table that evening, recalled in his sometimes unreliable 
Memoirs of Montparnasse. McAlmon, according to Glassco, “never ate anything.” 
Robert Menzies McAlmon (also used Robert M. McAlmon), as his signature name, March 
9, 1895 – February 2, 1956) was an American writer, poet, and publisher.[1] In the 1920s, 
he founded in Paris the publishing house, Contact Editions, where he  published Ernest 
Hemingway, Gertrude Stein, James Joyce and Ezra Pound.  McAlmon was born in 
Clifton, Kansas, the youngest of 10 children of an itinerant Presbyterian minister. He died 
in Desert Hot Springs, California at age 60. 
McAlmon studied for one semester as the University of Minnesota in 1916 before 
enlisting in the United States Army Air Corps in 1918. After World War I, he returned to 
university (1917–1920), this time at  University of Southern California. He attended 
classes intermittently until 1920, when he moved to Chicago and then New York City, 
where he worked as a nude model at an art school. Once in New York, he collaborated 
with William Carlos Williams on the Contact Review, which did not last for long but 
published poetry by Ezra Pound, Wallace Stevens, Marianne Moore, H.D., Kay Boyle, and 
Marsden Hartley. 
Robert McAlmon, 1928 


## Page 96

Kiki, “Queen of Montparnasse” 
Jose-Luis Bocquet and Catel Muller 
Celebrate Kiki’s Life Story in a Quirky Graphic Biography 
Alice Prin was the model known as Kiki, immortalized by myriad artists including Fernand Léger, Jean Cocteau, and her 
lover, Man Ray. It seems fitting that her latest biography should take a graphic form, in which she swoops across the pages 
alongside her friends and contemporaries Modigliani, Picasso, Matisse, Duchamp and Hemingway. 
The story of Prin could be told as a grim and cautionary tale – born illegitimate in Châtillon-sur-Seine in 1901, she died in equal 
poverty in 1953, after a lifetime of drink and drugs and debauchery. But this ribald comic strip version is as tender as it is witty and 
is a complex portrait of the model and muse. The author and artist, Jose- Luis Bocquet and Catel Muller – whose collaboration 
has already won several awards for the original French edition – resist the temptation to turn Kiki into either an emblematic 
victim of male objectification or the proud symbol of female emancipation. Instead, Kiki's contradictions emerge from the 
cartoons: her liberated sexual freedom is shadowed by a masochistic tendency to forgive abusive lovers; while her joyous 
embrace of the pleasures of life (food, art, wine, song, sunshine) is enmeshed with drug addiction and alcoholism. 
When Kiki's own memoirs were published in 1929 (and promptly banned in the United States), her friend Ernest Hemingway 
wrote an introduction that acknowledged her capacity for self-invention. "Having a fine face to start with she made of it a work 
of art . . . she certainly dominated that era of Montparnasse more than Queen Victoria ever dominated the Victorian era." Kiki's 
creativity was also apparent in the illustrations she sketched for her memoir, black and white drawings that shade between 
childlike innocence and something darker; and despite their surface naivety, her status as an artist, as well as a model, had 
already been consolidated with a sold-out exhibition of paintings in Paris in 1927. 
Nevertheless, the most enduringly famous image of Kiki is Man Ray's Le Violon d'Ingres, a photograph of her naked from 
behind, her signature bobbed hair hidden in a turban, her remarkable face almost hidden, and her voluptuous body.

Two Paintings by Modigliani recently attributed to model/muse Alice Prin - (c.) 1917 - more on pages  
"Lying Nude with Joined Hands" (c.) 1917 - Ceroni & "Unnamed" (c.) 1917
Below is an image of Modigliani's "Lying Nude with Hands Joined" (c.) 1917.  The Unnamed painting to the right is also by Modigliani and 
obvoiusly the same model.  After conducting a full evaluation of the available materials on the painting it is highly likely the subject of both 
these paintings was Modigliani's favorite musel, Alice Prin.  Convincing are features around the face, nose, mouth, eyebrows, bob haircut.  
These elements eerily consistent with Pacific Arts Collection's painting done of Alice in 1917.  Also similar is the title of our painting, "Sitting 
Nude with Crossed Hands" that matches an entry on the Berthe Weill 1917 exhibition.  Further similarities in both works are the ﬂeshy arms, 
breasts, hips and pubic hair.  Both are signed in black, bottom right.  Worth note the presence of the work in Ceroni means the publisher, 
Ambrogio Ceroni reviewed the painting, its provenance and had conversations and meetings with the painting's owner. 
1928


## Page 104

Kiki, "Queen of Montparnasse" 
The "Forgotten Star" - Early Years in Art 
Alice Ernestine Prin (2 October 1901 – 29 April 1953) - crowned KIKI, "Queen of Montparnasse" 1n 1929
Often known as Kiki de Montparnasse, was a French artist model, nightclub singer, actress, memoirist, and painter. She 
flourished in, and helped define, the liberated culture of Paris in the early 1920s.
Adopting a single name, "Kiki", she became a fixture in the Montparnasse social scene and a popular artist model, posing for 
dozens of artists, including Amedeo Modigliani, Chaim Soutine, "Julian Mandel" (a pseudonym), Tsuguharu Foujita, Francis 
Picabia, Jean Cocteau, Arno Breker, Alexander Calder, Per Krohg, Hermine David, Pablo Gargallo, Mayo, and Tono Salazar. Moise 
Kisling painted a portrait of Kiki titled Nu assis, one of his best known.
Her companion for most of the 1920s was Man Ray, who made hundreds of portraits of her. She is the subject of some of his 
best-known images, including the notable surrealist image Le violon d'Ingres and Noire et blanche.  She appeared in nine short 
and often experimental films, including Fernand Léger's Ballet mécanique without any credit.
A painter in her own right, in 1927 Prin had a sold-out exhibition of her paintings at the Galerie au Sacre du Printemps in Paris. 
Signing her work with her chosen single name, Kiki, she usually noted the year. Her drawings and paintings comprise portraits, 
self-portraits, social activities, fanciful animals, and dreamy landscapes composed in a light, slightly uneven, expressionist style 
that is a reflection of her easy-going manner and boundless optimism.
Her autobiography was published in 1929 as Kiki's Memoirs, with Ernest Hemingway and Tsuguharu Foujita providing 
introductions. In 1930 the book was translated by Samuel Putnam and published in Manhattan by Black Manikin Press, but it was 
immediately banned by the United States government.
A copy of the first US edition was held in the section for banned books in the New York Public Library through the 1970s. 
However, the book had been reprinted under the title The Education of a Young Model throughout the 1950s and 1960s (e. g.), a 
1954 edition by Bridgehead has the Hemingway Introduction and photos and illustrations by Mahlon Blaine). These editions were 
mainly put out by Samuel Roth. 
Taking advantage of the fact that the banning of the book meant it did not receive copyright protection in the U.S., Roth put out 
a series of supposedly copyrighted editions (which were never registered with the Library of Congress) which altered the text 
and added illustrations - line drawings and photographs - which were not by Prin. Editions published in and after 1955 include an 
extra 10 chapters supposedly written by Prin 23 years after the original book, including a visit to New York where she meets with 
Samuel Roth and Ernest Hemingway; none of this was true. The original autobiography finally saw a new translation and 
publication in 1996.


## Page 105

Pacific Arts Collection, who has contributed to this Expose is the exclusive representative of a Hidden Painting Treasure 
authored and signed by Amedeo Modigliani (c.) 1917).  The model is Alice Earnestine Prin, age 16.  The painting is titled "Sitting 
Nude with Crossed Hands".  Pacific Arts Collection has completed a 4+ year investigation, authentication, and forensic analysis of 
this painting gem.   The work was originally sold in Paris by Modigliani’s dealer, Léopold Zborowski and was kept in one family for 72 
years. This fact is well documented by notarized records, affidavits of family members, lawyers, and friends. 
Alice, at the age of 15 would meet, pose nude,  & become the favorite muse for Moïse Kisling, Modigliani, Man Ray,  Foujita, Soutine, 
Utrillo, and many others confessing, “the painters adopted me, the end of sad times.” She acquired the first half of her nickname in 
1922 after meeting Man Ray.  Kiki would then become the essential interlocutor for two generations of French Master's.  For over 
a decade, no one was more influential in bohemian circles of the Montparnasse Neighborhood of Paris. Her legacy and namesake 
became the inspiration for a modern-day successful line of Luxury Lingerie & Related Goods from "Kiki de Montparnasse"
This link, “Sitting Nude with Crossed Hands" - Videos - Bank of Commerce in Prague opens a Dropbox folder containing three 
videos, (each under 2 minutes) taken at the bank in Prague where the painting rests in a bank vault.  The persons in the first video 
include a research assistant, a friend of the owner, and Pacific Arts founder recording the video. In the first video, the viewer can 
see the canvas under black light and x-ray camera. The video highlights close ups of the canvas, frame, tack holes and Modigliani's 
signature. 
A second video, which we think is quite revealing, shows the back of the painting, and highlights multiple stamps/seals on the 
canvas and frame that are critical to the painting’s Provenance.  This video zooms in on the back of the canvas showing clearly 
that the canvas is consistent with other canvases including the thread count compared with canvases from the same year.  The 
third video shows the painting rotating with close ups of Alice and key parts of the paintings background. 
This link, InsightART-2022 Forensic Presentation opens another Dropbox Folder containing two 25-minute video presentations   
hosted by Jiri Lauterkranc cofounder InsightART - We Measure the DNA of Your Art.   The videos were created after a radiographic 
scan using particle physics technology, created by CERN, used by NASA & highlight Modigliani’s signature, style and geometry.  
Examples of their work;  BBC – Click Technology Series on Fine Art;  InsightArt - 2023 Radiographic  Scan - Marc Chagall - Female 
Nude with Flowers & Animals This Chagall is also represented by Pacific Arts Collection .  A third example; InsightART X- ray 
Technology Reveals  Hidden Secrets of "Card Players "by Jakub Schikaneder. 
On page 13 of this Report is Exhibit #11 from Pacific Arts Collections Confirmation Clues Report.  The Exhibit focuses on the brown 
circles around parts of Kiki's face, especially the eyes, a signature style of Modigliani.  There have been two additional forensic 
evaluations of the painting done in 2011 by Art-Test and 2014 by ARS-Mensrae.  Both came to the same conclusions as InsightART, 
confirming pigment, canvas, frame and signature.  All three paint tests reveal no use of titanium white, common in most forgeries.  
The painting of Alice was done  with  lead white and  zinc white as was common for Modigliani.

## Page 106

For Immediate Release....
Pacific Arts Collection is pleased to announce the exclusive offering of Amedeo Modigliani's Sitting Nude with Crossed 
Hands (c. 1917).  A newly authenticated masterwork featuring Alice Ernestine Prin at age 16—later known to the world as 
Kiki de Montparnasse  in 1922 after meeting Man Ray.  Ultimately Kiki was  crowned Kiki, "Queen of Montparnasse” in a 
lavish ceremony in 1929.  Earnest Hemingway wrote the forward to Kiki's Memoirs, one of two he would do in his life.
This extraordinary painting captures Kiki on the threshold of legend—before the fame, the salons, and the mythos that 
came to define Montparnasse in the roaring 1920s. With his signature red lake effect, violent eyes and haunting emotional 
realism, Modigliani presents her in a pose that is intimate and timeless, rendered with lyrical restraint and psychological 
depth.
In 2022, a comprehensive forensic analysis conducted by InsightART in Prague—employing advanced CERN particle 
physics robotic imaging—confirmed the materials, layering, signature, canvas, frame and brushwork as authentic to 
Modigliani’s oeuvre.  A Verification Report and Authorization Letter were recently issued by Dr. Otto M. Urban, former 
curator of the National Museum in Prague and a world renowned expert in early modern art.
This is the first time this Modigliani Nude has ever been offered for private sale.  It is not only a singular rediscovered work 
by one of the most revered artists of the 20th century—it is also a vital contribution to the visual legacy of Kiki de 
Montparnasse, who inspired an entire generation of artists, poets, and revolutionaries in Inter-war Paris.
Private and confidential discussions are welcome, for acquisition or exhibition inquiries please contact:
Pacific Arts Collection
Richard Triberg
President - Research Specialist
415-233-3131
richard@localheros.net

## Page 107

Title: 
Author: 
Date: 
Sitting Nude with Hands Crossed
Modigliani 
1917 
Signature: 
Yes 
Medium: 
Oil on Canvas 
Dimensions: 40.5 x 33 cm 
Source: 
Owner:
Private Coll. Prague 
Upon Inquiry/ NDA 
Description 
The painting being examined depicts a seated female nude sitting on a 
reddish pillow with greenish tassel trim in a darkened room. The seated 
female figure has her legs crossed and both arms joined below her left knee. 
The whole scene is set in the interior of the room, which is painted in dark 
shades of brown, ochre and green. A still life with a table and chair is painted 
in the upper right corner. There is a partial white tablecloth visible. On the left 
side lower edge is a reddish-brown drape. In the upper left side leading into 
the top center is what appears to be a wooden cabinet with a bowl and a gray 
looking cover on top of the cabinet. In the lower right corner is the signature 
“modigliani” in black paint. 
Research Methodology 
This examination was carried out based on requirement to verify the 
authenticity and style of the artwork in terms of technological and artistic 
quality. This work of art has already been the subject of several exhaustive 
technological analyses in the past (2011-Rome, 2014-Florence – see Exhibits), 
focusing mainly on its material composition, technical condition and 
subsequent restoration interventions. The subject of this study is therefore 
also a comparison of all previous results and their complementation with new 
findings and analyses. This work also includes a comparison of the artist's 
painting style, technique, materials, and signature. As part of the research, 
detailed photo-documentation of the front and back of the painting in visible 
daylight was carried out, as well as macro and micro images and photographs 
in bright side lighting (raking light). A verification image of Infrared 
Reflectography and Ultraviolet Luminescence was also taken. 
This work also includes X-Ray images using InsightART's RToo scanning 
technology. Drawn from CERN technology and used by NASA, the RToo 
provides pseudo-color X-ray scans, revealing differences in material 
compositions. Additional information is included in exhibits to this report. 
Results from previous scans and testing are compared and commented upon 
in this report. 
As part of the research, detailed photo-documentation was carried out, including macro and micro images, photographs 
with raking light, and verification images of Infrared Reflectography and Ultraviolet Luminescence. This work also includes X-ray 
images using scanning technology drawn from CERN technology and used by NASA. The technology provides pseudo-color 
X-ray scans, revealing differences in material compositions. Results from previous scans and testing are compared and
commented upon in the complete report.
Signature - Radiographic Analysis and Comparison: 
The signature was extensively examined for the purpose of its authenticity and belonging to the layers of painting itself. A 
survey of the material composition of the individual letters of the signature was carried out, which confirmed the presence 
of ivory black. The possibility of the occurrence of bone black with respect to trace amounts of other detected elements is 
also mentioned. Furthermore, the method of craquelure through individual letters of the signature was examined using a 
microscope. 
A radiographic analysis of the signatures was also performed and its comparison with other signatures from Modigliani's well- 
known works. See 2022 letter to the founder of InsightART, Jiri Lauterkranc and their Testing Report. The results of this 
survey show that there is a strong agreement on the basic characteristics of Modigliani's signature style. Based on these 
aspects, and similar data from 2011 and 2014 it can be stated that the signature is genuine. 
2022 Forensic Report - IInsightART - Excerpt
Sitting Nude with Hands Crossed
InsightART
Jiří Lauterkranc PhD
Prague 
2022

To whom it my concern:  
Attached is an affidavit by Mr. Svatopluk Bouska, which includes Czech 
and English versions. Included in the affidavit is a discussion by Mr. 
Bouska about  his 2012 visit to the Wildenstein Institute in Paris. He and

where he had a conversation with a member of the assessment 
committee. He was told that the committee was certain the painting was 
not a forgery but did not have a sufficient provenance to be considered 
for the forthcoming Catalogue Raisonne, at the time planned for 2014. 
Mrs. Hadamek followed up with confirming emails.
In 2017, the owner, Mr. Kuzmanovski retained  Pacific Arts Collection 
(PAC) to begin research work on the provenance and subject of the 
painting. In 2022 InsightART (Prague) performed a radiographic scan of 
the painting using particle physics (robotic) technology developed by 
CERN and utilized by NASA.  
The scan led to 100% confirmation of Modigliani's signature, canvas, 
paint/pigment, and frame. InsightART produced two 25-minute videos 
Hosted by founder Jiri Lauterkranc summarizing his findings that we 
highly recommend viewing.  A Dropbox link is provided in the Cover 
Letter.  Additional scans were done by Art-Test in 2014, and ARS 
Mensvrae in 2011 with the same confirming results.   The Report from 
InsightART is Addendum B to Pacific Arts Collection's Authentication & 
Analysis Report.
Pacific Arts Collection spent over 5 years in exhaustive effort across 
several countries documenting the painting's provenance. The findings 
are contained in the Research Report sections on "Closing Investigative 
Report, Certified Affidavits, Notarized Declarations & Family Letters."
The owner of the painting, Mr. Saso Kuzmanovski  was introduced to 
Pacific Arts Collection after he was informed of their over 2 decades of 
research work completed  authenticating Emile Bernard's "The Passion of 
JESUS-CHRIST at Calvary" (c.) 1926-40, 270 x 193 cm.  This investigation led 
to the paintings inclusion in Jean-Jacques Luthi & Armand Israel 2014 
Bernard Catalogue Raisonne.  
Nude woman seated

41 x 33 cm

## Page 113

  Maurice Mendjisky   Polish Artist - 1889-1951 - 
His father was a tinsmith. From an early age, he was gifted in artistic 
disciplines.  Initially passionate about music, he left at a young age for Berlin 
to study at a composition school. But, an excellent drawer, he finally became 
interested in painting and left for Paris . Between 1906 and 1907, he briefly 
spent time in Fernand Cormon's studio at the École des Beaux-Arts in Paris . 
He quickly left the studio for a freer life where he worked a lot and was 
quickly noticed by the great dealers of the time. He exhibited at Georges Petit 
in 1912. The preface to the catalogue is by André Salmon.  In the same years, 
he met Léopold Zborowski, one of the great art dealers of the time and 
became friends with Amedeo Modigliani, Pablo Picasso, Auguste Renoir. 
In fact, it was in 1913 that he met Renoir. The latter regularly invited him to his 
home at Les Collettes, in Cagnes-sur-Mer. Enthusiastic about the light and 
atmosphere of the Côte d'Azur, Maurice Mendjizky encouraged his comrades, 
Modigliani, Chaïm Soutine, Léonard Foujita to join him. After a stay in Poland 
and Russia, he returned to Paris and in 1918 met Alice Prin who would later 
become the model Kiki de Montparnasse. In love, they lived as a couple for 
three years until 1922.  In 1921, he participated in the first exhibition organized 
at the Café du Parnasse by Auguste Clergé and Serge Romoff. But after his 
breakup with Kiki, he left Paris for the South and took refuge in his work.

## Page 115

Man Ray 2 Iconic RARE Videos Staring "Kiki" Circa 1926 
Man Ray & Kiki 1922 Montparnasse Neighborhood  
Man Ray Photos taken of "Kiki" 1921-1929 
These photographs depict the image of Kiki as having a “large and splendid” body. The wide hips and thick upper thighs continue throughout 
her illustrations and paintings. Later in life, her girth increased until the legendary French photographer, Man Ray was instrumental in getting 
Kiki to lose significant weight. This image makeover, (in her early 20's ) helped to enhance her illustrious career on the cabaret circuit in and 
around Paris. Homeless in her teens, Kiki would expose herself to strangers for francs in alleyways to help make ends meet. 
L'étoile de mer, Man Ray, 1928  YouTube - Film-man 
"L'Étoile de mer", "The Starfish" is a 1928 film directed by 
Man Ray and based on a short poem and longer scenario, 
both written by Robert Desnos. The film depicts a couple 
(played by Alice Prin, a.k.a. "Kiki de Montparnasse" and 
André de la Rivière) acting through scenes that are shot out 
of focus, and with Desnos himself as the second man in the 
final scene.  Almost all of the scenes in this film are shot 
either off a mirror like the final shot, or through what 
appears to be diffused and textured glass. After opening to 
the couple walking along a road, the scene cuts to a caption, 
".Les dents des femmes sont des objets si charmants" 
"women's teeth are such charming things" then scene 
where the "Kiki" alters her stocking.  "that one ought to see 
them only in a dream or in the instant of love".  From this 
point the couple retire to the upper bedroom of a house and 
the female undresses and retires, at which point the male 
bids her farewell.
Man Ray Emak Bakia, 1926 -- starring “Kiki de Montparnasse” Version 1 - 16-min 
Man Ray Emak Bakia , 1926, starring “Kiki de Montparnasse” Version 2 - 20-min 
Emak Bakia Basque for Leave me Alone, duration 19:44. The full title is Emak 
Bakia (CInépoème) (Fichez-moi la paix is a 1926 French  surrealist short film 
directed by Man Ray. Termed as a cinépoème (cinematic poem) by its author, it 
features many techniques Man Ray used in his still photography (for which he is 
better known), including Rayographs, double exposure, soft focus and 
ambiguous features. Synopsis: Emak Bakia shows elements of fluid mechanical 
motion in parts, rotating artifacts showing his ideas of everyday objects being 
extended and rendered useless. Kiki “Queen of Montparnasse Alice Prin is 
shown driving a car in a scene through a town. Towards the middle of the film 
Jacques Rigaut appears dressed in female clothing and make-up. Later in the 
film a caption appears, “La raison de cette extravagance” “the reason for this 
extravagance”. The film then cuts to a car arriving and a passenger leaving with 
briefcase entering a building, opening the case revealing men's shirt collars 
which he proceeds to tear in half. The collars are then used as a focus for the 
film, rotating through double exposures. The film features sculptures by Pablo 
Picasso, and some of Man Ray's mathematical objects both still and animated 
using a stop motion technique.

## Page 117

Moïse Kisling {born Mojżesz Kisling} - January 22 1891 – April 29 1953 was a Polish-born French painter.[1][2] Born in Kraków, then 
part of Austria-Hungary, to Jewish parents, Kisling studied at the Academy of Fine Arts. He left for Paris in 1910 at the age of 19. 
After moving to Montmartre, Kisling became a member of the Parisian avant-garde known also as the School of Paris, and 
developed close professional relationships with painters Amedeo Modigliani and Jules Pascin, among others. Kisling gained 
recognition for portraying the female form and completed numerous nudes and portraits during his career. 
He became a French national in 1924, after serving and being wounded with the French Foreign Legion in World War I. In 1940, 
despite being 49, Kisling rejoined the army for World War II but moved to the United States following the French Army's 
surrender and the impending threat to Jews in occupied France. In the U.S., he exhibited his works in New York City and 
Washington and settled in Southern California. After World War II and the defeat of Nazi Germany, Kisling returned to France. He 
lived his later years continuing his artwork until his death in 1953, after a brief illness.

## Page 132

Exhibit 11 from 84-pg Authentication & Analysis Report 
Confirming Modigliani  Painting Style & Unique Techniques 
The most interesting discovery followed an investigation of the 
underpainting, i. e. how the artist prepared his composition. They 
discovered that details of the face, nose, mouth, and eyes were uniformly 
drawn in “large and violent” rings of brown paint. These circular 
brushworks were so unvarying that this was a conclusive way to identify 
Modigliani’s hand. Genuine works always showed this artistic signature; 
faked works never did.  
The reader can see these brown circles are on this painting! That is as good 
as the signature of Modigliani being confirmed by three testing services as 
original and in “bone black” pigment and signed at the painting 
completion. Other details supporting this painting as a “style” by 
Modigliani are the lines and the body parts as discussed in items 9,10, and 
11. This is yet another “atypical, but genuine” painting by Modigliani, similar 
to the Diego Rivera that was confirmed using the same analysis data.
Excerpts from Meryle Secrest  2011 Book “Modigliani A Life” pages 330 & 
331
Amedeo Modigliani 
"When I know your soul, I will paint your eyes"
Amedeo Modigliani—this quote of the famous painter still invokes shivers among 
his followers.  What he captures so vividly is the bridge between elements of past 
centuries and modern art.  Modigliani was known for his distinct style—elongated 
faces and figures with flat, almond-shaped eyes that often-lacked pupils. 
Emotional Depth: He believed the eyes were the gateway to the soul.
The "Blank" Eyes: Many of his portrait’s feature "blind" eyes because he felt he hadn't 
yet truly understood the sitter's inner essence.
Jeanne Hébuterne: He eventually painted his common-law wife, Jeanne, with clearly 
defined pupils, signaling he had finally "seen" her soul.
Moments from movie, Modigliani (2004) by Mick Davis, starring Andy Garcia as Amedeo 
Modigliani and Elsa Zylberstein as Jeanne Hébuterne - set to music by Guy Farley


## Page 133

Two Paintings by Amedeo Modigliani recently attributed to model/muse, Alice Earnestine Prin - 1917
"Lying Nude with Joined Hands" & "Unnamed"
Below is an image of Modigliani's "Lying Nude with Hands Joined" (c.) 1917.  The unnamed painting to the right is also by 
Modigliani and obvoiusly the same model.  After conducting a full evaluation of the available materials on the painting it is 
highly likely the subject of both these paintings was Modigliani's favorite musel, Alice Prin.  Convincing are features around the 
face, nose, mouth, eyebrows, bob haircut.  These elements eerily consistent with Pacific Arts Collection's painting done of Alice 
in 1917.  Also similar is the title of our painting, "Sitting Nude with Crossed Hands" that matches an entry on the Berthe Weill 
1917 exhibition.  Further similarities in both works are the ﬂeshy arms, breasts, hips and pubic hair.  Both are signed in black, 
bottom right.
The current owner of the as yet uncatalogued work is in the process of contacting the owner of the painting to see if there are 
any additional clues to the Subject.  "Lying Nude with Joined Hands" is also listed in Pantani, Lanthemann Parisot, Restellini, 
Wayne, and Pfanstiel.  Worth note, these four catalogs have limited entries and are missing other paintings by Modigliani. The 
presence of the work in Ceroni means the publisher, Ambrogio Ceroni reviewed the painting, its provenance and had 
conversations and meetings with the painting's owner. 
Modigliani - Unnamed - 1917
Above middle: 1928 photo of Kiki taken a few days before she was officially 
crowned, Kiki, "Queen of Montparnasse" - Note characteristics in image 
above that are similar to the subject painting of this Addendum Report.
•
Kiki's signature shaped lips, slightly extended lipstick line  -  seen in numerous 
photos/paintings of Alice
•
Body type, hands, finders, nose, navel all are consistent with other closeup 
photography and painting by over a dozen artists
•
Kiki had a condition that prevented growth of pubic hair - a fact not widely 
circulated in artistic circles.  She talks about this condition in her Memoirs: 
Chapter XIV My First Appearance in Artist Circles 1917 - " Posing is something I 
am not very crazy about, for I've got a pilose system that's not as well 
developed as it might be in a certain spot, and so, I have to dab myself up with 
black chalk. But I can give a swell imitation of hair"
Chapter XX, 1922 - Foujita - "I also posed for Foujita. The thing that astonished 
him about me the most was the lack of hair on my sexual parts".  Page 133 
Note:  there is a scene in a French Animated Film, where Foujita makes light of 
this body anomaly.  It is well known that Kiki asked all the artists and 
photographers she modeled for to make accommodations to keep her secret. 
The artists obliged although several photographers did not follow her 
instruction and did not retouch the negatives, including three shown right 
taken by Julian Mandel 1917-18.

## Page 134

Left - Painting is covered with a high glossy varnish layer, done by the 
artist, it is recorded in several sources that Modigliani wanted to imitate 
the feel & texture of the old masters, a modern Titian, so he varnished his 
painting to achieve a similar glossy craquelure of the surface  (also some of 
the newest studies have discovered some other were varnished later). 
This is one of the main reasons to avoid strong cleaning in the Modigliani 
nudes, first because they lost the red/orange (red lake) veils mixed with 
the varnish.  I have no information of any cleaning procedure in this case, 
but the surface seems to be clean and the painting when live maintain the 
reds in perfect shape (many known photos adjust the red surface to a 
more softer orange or almost pink in photographic effects).” 
Note:  Subject Painting of Alice Prin has these exact same traits. Pacific 
Arts Collection’ is soon going to undertake a cleaning of the work.  
Removing accumulated dust, dirt and slight imperfections of the lower 
right.  It will NOT include removal of any varnish no matter how light, even 
what Slansky did in his restoration work. Wiki also points out the four tack 
holes.  Our painting has the same 4 tack holes.

Tate Gallery Modern - British Paintings, Drawings & Sculptures, Volume II - Artists M-Z - pages 479-480 
"Based on a sketch made in the Paris studio of the author and journalist Sisley Huddleston, who, when the painting was first placed 
on view at the Tate Gallery, protested in a letter to the Paris edition of the New York Herald Tribune, 24 February 1927 (reprinted in 
many English newspapers), that the artist had introduced a nude figure not in the original sketch. The nude, standing by the window, 
not reclining on the couch as asserted by Sisley Huddleston, was, however, already in the picture when it was exhibited at the 
Leicester Galleries in March 1926 (reproduction printed March 3, 1926 in "The Sketch Newspaper"). The artist did make certain 
alterations before the painting was presented to Tate Gallery, but these consisted of the elimination of a bearded artist (quite unlike 
the Shakespeare-like appearance of Sisley Huddleston) at the easel, the looping up of the curtains which originally hung straight to 
show windows continuing on either side, and the addition of the book on the left-hand edge of the table". 
Published - Mary Chamot, Dennis Farr and Martin Butlin, The Modern British Paintings, Drawings and Sculpture, London 1964, II 
Other  -  A copy of artist, Richard Nevinson’s sketch (referenced above) is shown below.  Based on numerous clues, circled on 
image on pg. _.   It is more than circumstantial that the artist depicted on the bench in front of the painting is Amedeo Modigliani. 
The female nude is strongly believed to be Alice Prin, who Modigliani met at “Chez Rosalie’s” in 1915. There he often sketched 
Alice and sold the sketches to patrons.  Several of Modigliani’s sketches of Alice are cataloged, in private collections & gallery's.  
Alice Prin, (Jewish decent) would become known as "Kiki de Montparnasse" in 1922 after meeting Man Ray, Kiki, "Queen of 
Montparnasse" in 1929.

## Page 140

Chapter 7 -  The Lost Studio of Montparnasse 
The painting had hung in the Tate Gallery for nearly a century, silent and unassuming. No crowds gathered around it. No art 
historians clambered to decode its meaning. It was simply there—An Artist Studio in Montparnasse, by British painter Christopher 
Richard Wynne Nevinson. 
But beneath the layers of oil and pigment, a secret had been hiding. A secret so profound that, if true, it would rewrite a piece of 
art history. It took over 2,500 hours of relentless research—scouring archives, cross-referencing sketches, and unearthing 
forgotten letters—to uncover the truth. 
And the truth? 
This wasn’t just a painting of an artist’s studio. It was a painting of the studio—the Montparnasse studio shared by Nevinson and 
Amedeo Modigliani. 
A scandalous realization. Because if that was Modigliani in the painting… then the nude figure standing by the window could only 
be Alice Prin—better known as "Kiki de Montparnasse"  a title given to Alice in 1922 at the age of 22, under the tutelage of Man 
Ray, who she had a stormy decade long affair.
The greatest muse of the Parisian avant-garde. 
The pieces had been hiding in plain sight. A red velvet pillow, seen in multiple photographs.   A white shawl draped over a chair, a 
signature from Modigliani’s 1917 nude portraits, appearing in dozens of his works. And a forgotten letter, buried in the pages of 
history, accusing Nevinson of altering the painting before it was presented to Tate. 
But why? 
Why would Nevinson try to erase the past? 
A Shared Studio, A Hidden Past 
The world remembers Nevinson as a bold painter, a modernist who captured the grit of war and industry. But few knew that, for 
two years, he had shared a studio with Modigliani in the heart of Montparnasse. 
The revelation came like a shock-wave. A seated figure in the painting—long assumed to be just another artist—bore a haunting 
resemblance to Modigliani. The angular jawline, the placement of the ear against the thick beard, the way he slouched, lost in his 
work. 
Modigliani had lived in Montparnasse at a time when the world was against him. He was poor, drinking too much, his health 
failing. But he was also a genius, producing work at a feverish pace. And next to him?  Nevinson. 
Watching. Sketching. Absorbing. 
It had been so obvious, yet so overlooked. 
But there was one detail in the painting that held the key to it all. 
The nude figure by the window. 
Alice Prin - The Muse Who Owned Paris 
Alice Prin was thrown out of her mother’s home at twelve years old. Alone in Paris, she survived on the streets, taking any job, 
she could—until an artist found her and put a pencil to paper. 
By 1922, she was legendary.  Officially crowned  KIKI, "Queen of Montparnasse" in 1929 in a lavish ceremony. A muse to the 
greatest artists of her time—Man Ray, Fujita, Kisling, Calder, Hemingway and perhaps most famously….Modigliani. 


## Page 141

It was no secret that Modigliani adored her. He sketched her obsessively. He saw something in her that no one else did—
something raw, untamed, almost electric. 
And so when the research team examined Nevinson’s painting, there she was. The same bobbed hair. The same posture.  
manner of her arms.  The same red velvet pillow seen in multiple photos and under Alice  when Modigliani painted her in 1917. 
And yet… when Nevinson’s painting was first placed on view at the Tate Gallery in 1927, the controversy erupted. 
Sisley Huddleston, a well-connected journalist, wrote a scathing letter to the New York Herald Tribune. He claimed Nevinson had 
added a nude figure to the painting that hadn’t been in the original sketch. But research proved otherwise—the nude had 
always been there. 
Huddleston wasn’t upset that a nude had been added. He was upset because he knew exactly who she was. 
Alice Prin
The girl who had once been a street urchin. The girl who had grown into the untouchable icon of Montparnasse. The girl whose 
image, immortalized in Nevinson’s painting, linked her to Modigliani in a way no one had ever realized before. 
The Secret Edits That Changed History 
But something happened between the painting’s first exhibition in 1926 and its arrival at Tate in 1927. Nevinson changed it. 
•
He removed the bearded artist, who else could it be but for Modigliani
•
He looped up the curtains, revealing more of the windows
•
He altered the basic beige fabric of the curtains in favor of heavy dark velvet, likely red
•
Curtains that originally hung straight to show windows continuing on either side
•
Which version was accurate? - likely the velvet - depicted in many of his nudes
•
He added the book on the left-hand edge of the table - a distraction, an attempt to shift focus
Why? 
The answer, researchers believe, is simple - fear. 
Modigliani’s reputation was already infamous. His name was tied to scandal, to wild nights and tragic endings. But Alice? She 
was something else entirely. 
She was still alive. Still famous. Still a fire-brand. At the peak of her stardom.  Crowned KIKI, Queen of Montparnasse in 1929 in a 
lavish ceremony.
And in 1926, a painting of Modigliani, sitting in a studio next to a teenage Alice, would have raised too many questions. Perhaps 
Nevinson worried that people would recognize the girl in the painting. Perhaps he feared the Leicester Galleries would refuse 
to continue to show it. Perhaps he simply didn’t want to be caught in the storm that was sure to follow. 
So, he did what any artist under pressure might do. 
He edited history.

Cont from pg 121 - The Final Clue 
There was one last detail that sealed the case. A March 3, 1926, issue of The 
Sketch Newspaper contained a color reproduction of the painting before it 
was altered. In it, the nude figure is not standing by the window—
but reclining on the couch. 
Exactly as Sisley Huddleston had originally claimed
Nevinson had removed the artist. He had adjusted the composition. But he 
had never been able to erase the truth. 
And now, nearly a century later, the truth was out.  An Artist Studio in 
Montparnasse was never just an ordinary painting. 
It was a hidden portrait of Amedeo Modigliani and Alice Prin, frozen 
in time, locked away in the Tate Gallery for decades, waiting for 
someone to see what had been there, all along
a love story, a scandal, a secret, a painting that refused to be forgotten 
A hidden portrait of the two, an intimate setting, frozen in time
 locked away in Tate Gallery for decades, 
waiting for somone to see what had been there all along 


## Page 143

Could in be that Nevinson  experienced serious blow-back from Leicester Gallery at the scene of a nude model half his age on 
the couch in in the studio he shared with Modigliani.  The gallery must have known the artist in the painting on the bench was 
indeed Modigliani, certainly Nevinson knew the model was Alice Prin.  None of Modigliani's other nudes had the distinctive bob 
haircut.  The body-type is consistent will photography from the same period.   
Pacific Arts Collection will soon receive a copy of the March 3, 1926, "Sketch Newspaper” that printed a color image of the 
painting with the nude model in Nevinson's painting reclining on the couch as Sisley Huddleston proclaimed.  We are also trying 
to locate the Feb 24, 1927 Paris edition of the New York Herald Tribune and “many English newspapers” that Sisley Huddlestons 
letter was printing in.  He of course an eye-witness to the original sketch done in his Paris studio.
Further, Modigliani's signature white shawl in over two dozen of his 1917 Nudes is draped over the chair behind the nude model. 
The green hat behind the shawl, (top right of chair) is similar to the style of hat that Alice wore, see page 22 .  The canvases and 
satchel scattered on the floor behind artist bench was typical of Modigliani. The furniture is consistent with many photo's of 
Modi's other Monparnasse studios.  Also in many of his nude paintings from 1917 a red couch is present. 
No need to stretch ones imagination to realize that the artist that Nevinson removed from his painting could not be any other 
artist of the period other than Modigliani.  Keeping in mind the two of them shared a Studio in Montparnasse for 2 years. 
Certainly no one but Modigliani painted in the style depicted in the painting resting up against the easel.  There are numerous 
examples in Dr. Paul Alexandre's book, the "Unknown Modigliani" and are included further in this Report.

Rue #3 Joseph Bara in Monparnasse: where Zabrowski lived with his partner 
Hanka.  It is well documented that he rented a Studio for Modigliani in 1917 in 
the same building and supplied him with models.  Alice Prin refers to 
Zabrowski in her Memoirs.  It is also known that in 1917 at this Studio most all 
of Modi's nude portraits were painted. Zabrowski wrote his address on the 
back of several of Modigliani's canvases in 1917 in anticipation of the Berthe 
Weill Exhibition.  So the painting could be returned after the exhibition or if 
there were any disruptions. 
Quote from Kiki's 1929 First Edition Memoirs, Chapter XIX "Posing" - 1918-19 
"Kisling's promised not to bawl me out any more.  He's given me a contract 
for three months.  But most of the time I'm a mess as a model.  He yells at 
the top of his voice to make me laugh, or else-----makes all sorts of funny 
noises, and we try to see who can outdo the other.  A great little playmate"  
"Zborowski climbs the stairs several times in the course of the morning, just 
to get an eyeful and see how things are coming along"  


## Page 147

Amedeo Modigliani  Caryatid - 1915-16
Gouache and watercolor with 
 chalk and graphite on wove paper 
Modigliani made more than seventy drawings of 
caryatids. Their highly stylised manner shows his 
absorption in a wide variety of arts then considered to be 
‘primitive’, including African and especially Cambodian 
carvings. The drawings were preparatory sketches for 
sculptures, and Modigliani is said to have conceived of a 
‘temple to humanity’ surrounded by hundreds of such 
caryatids. However, he appears to have made only one 
carving directly related to this crouching figure. 


## Page 148

Artist: 
Amedeo Modigliani (Italian, 1884–1920) 
Culture: 
Italian 
Titles: 
Caryatid - has been attributed t0 Kiki de Montparnasse
Date: (c.) 1915-16 
Place: Paris, France 
Medium:  Gouache and watercolor with chalk and graphite on wove paper 
Dimensions:  Sheet: 55 3/8 × 26 3/16 in. (140.7 × 66.5 cm) 
Frame (outer): 63 1/4 × 34 × 2 5/8 in. (160.7 × 86.4 × 6.7 cm) 
Credit Line Gift of Oveta Culp Hobby 
Object number 84.412 
Explore Further 
Prints and Drawings 
Object Type: 
DRAWINGS, PASTELS & WATERCOLORS 
Description: 
Amedeo Modigliani came to Paris in 1906, aspiring to become a sculptor. He befriended Constantin Brancusi, assimilating his 
treatment of simplified forms while retaining a greater naturalism. About 1913, Modigliani began to envision a “temple of 
humanity” that would feature monumental caryatid figures carved from stone, based on archetypes found in Classical 
architecture. He made numerous studies, of which this example is one of the largest and most painterly. Lack of funds put an 
end to this project, however, and Modigliani gave up sculpture for painting 
shortly thereafter.   
Provenance: 
Oveta Culp Hobby, Houston, by November 19, 1984; given to MFAH, 1984 
Exhibition History: 
"Amedeo Modigliani," Kunstsammlung Nordrhein-Wesrfalen, Dusseldorf, January 19–April 7,1991; Kunsthaus Zurich, April 19–July 21, 
1991. 
"Amedeo Modigliani Retrospective," The Jewish Museum, New York, May 21–September 19, 2004; The Art Gallery of Ontario, 
Toronto, October 23, 2004–January 23, 2005. 
"Against the Current: 20th Century Representational Art in the Collection of the MFAH," The Museum of Fine Arts, Houston, October 
7, 2006–September 2, 2007. 
"Europe 1900-1975: Selections from the Museum's Collection," The Museum of Fine Arts, Houston, March 4, 2014–January 4, 2015. 
"Modigliani Unmasked," The Jewish Museum, New York, September 17, 2017–February 4, 2018.  Catalogue raisonné 
 Pfannstiel 68

Christopher Richard Wynne Nevinson, A.R.A. (1889-1946) 
Sur la Terrasse, Montparnasse 
signed 'C R W NEVINSON' (lower right) oil on canvas 
24 x 30 in. (61 x 76.2 cm.) 
Painted in 1925.

The artist, until 1937.

## Page 153

SPECIAL NOTICE: 
No VAT will be charged on the hammer price, but VAT at 17.5% will be added to the buyer's premium, which is invoiced on a VAT 
inclusive basis. 
LOT ESSAY: 
Dating from 1925, the present work depicts Parisian café society and was painted during Nevinson's most productive period. It 
was painted a year after Sunday evening, punts of the Thames at Henley (sold in these rooms, 30 November 2000, lot 35, for a 
world record price, £201,750, private collection) and both paintings display elements of Nevinson's return to pre-war post-
impressionism. 
Although after the war, Nevinson had declared that he had given up futurism, Sur la Terrasse, Montparnasse is indebted to 
Nevinson's involvement with the Futurist movement, displayed in the striking diagonals shooting beyond the electric lights 
depicted. 'Electric lights recur frequently in Nevinson's work, as they did in the work of many of the Futurists; Nevinson recalls in 
Paint and Prejudice the ﬁrst time he saw a light bulb when staying at a hotel in Paris with his mother before the War. Visually, he 
was clearly as much interested in what they hid as what they revealed, thereby equating them with a complex set of values' (R. 
Ingleby (et. al.), loc. cit). 
The present work is readable across the picture plane, however, there is a fragmentation of the subject matter that is typical of 
Nevinson's compositions. The clustering confusion of heads that are crammed into the conﬁnes of the canvas convey to the 
viewer the cacophony of noise that must have risen from these outdoor cafés. The sharp diagonals descending from the lights 
are echoed in the roofs of the buildings and brought forward into the lower half of the painting, holding the crowd of people in a 
snapshot of stillness within their mass of sociableness.

## Page 155

Sisley Huddleston (28 May 1883 – 14 July 1952) was a British journalist and writer. After editing a British forces newspaper in the First 
World War, he was resident in Paris after the war until the 1930’s, writing for The Times (London) and the Christian Science 
Monitor.  In his Europe in Zigzags (1929) he supported the Pan-Europe manifesto of Richard von Coudenhove-Kalergi.[1] War Unless 
(1933) was a "deliberately alarmist" [2] call for revision of the Treaty of Versailles.
During the Second World War, he was in Vichy France, taking French citizenship and writing in sympathy with the regime.[3] He 
interviewed Marshal Philippe Pétain.  He was arrested in October 1944 by French authorities on treason charges.[4]  He was 
imprisoned by the Free French, in 1944 as a Vichy collaborator.[5] He wrote a number of works that were critical in particular of 
the Allied handling of the Liberation of France and politicians' diplomacy.  
Free download of Book -  Those Europeans - Study of Foreign Faces -1924

•

•
France and the French, C. Scribner's Sons, 1925.
•

•

## Page 156

Sisley Huddleston 
drawn in Paris, c. 1925 
pastel with pencil 51.2 x 34.2 cm 
signed l.r., pencil “Stella Bowen”, not dated 
inscribed l.c., pencil “Sisley Huddleston” - private collection 
Sisley Huddleston (1883–1952) was an English writer who adopted France as his home. Posted to Paris during the First 
World War, Huddleston became an expert in French politics and history. He also found time to socialise with and write 
about the literary and artistic expatriates of Paris, including Ford and Bowen. In fact, he was so often to be found at the 
famous English-language bookshop, “Shakespeare and Co.” that he was dubbed “Mr. Shakespeare”. 
Huddleston was ambivalent about the social and political changes he witnessed in his beloved France after the First World 
War and came to sympathise with the emerging right wing movements of the 1930’s. During the German occupation he 
remained in France and was a vocal supporter of the collaborationist Vichy Government, leading to his imprisonment after 
the Allied liberation. However, always a journalist, Huddleston continued to write throughout his public rise and fall.

## Page 159

Valerie Bougault - Paris Montparnasse: The Heyday of Modern Art 1910-1940 [Glorious Art] 


## Page 160

In the interwar years, in burgeoning Montparnasse, artists searched relentlessly to capture the image 
reflected by the human face.  Each in his own fashion sought to reconstruct, in compelling portraits
human representation, Matisse and Chagall set their paintings ablaze with pure color, Soutine deployed 
naked expressiveness, Modigliani mastered the nude, while Picasso chose the road of deconstruction. The 
creative individuality inspired freedom, a freedom which was to imbue Montparnasse with a unique 
atmosphere and astound the visitors who flocked from the world over to discover its magic.
