## Page 220

What has been overlooked in analyses of the caption is the veiled reference to one of 
the most famous poems of the nineteenth century, “La Chevelure” (Hair), from Charles 
Baudelaire’s masterpiece, Fleurs du mal (h e Flowers of Evil). In his great love poem to 
Jeanne Duval, the woman contemporaries referred to as his mulatto mistress (his mother 
called her the “Black Venus”), Baudelaire sinks into the ecstasy of smelling and touch-
ing his mistress’s hair. In a manner similar to the text in Vogue, hair becomes a point of 
departure for an imaginary sensual voyage to an exotic, untrammeled state of “primitiv-
ism.” h e second verse begins:
Languorous Asia and burning Africa,
h e whole of a distant world, almost extinct,
Lives in your depths, aromatic forest!
As other spirits drift upon music,
Mine, O my love! ﬂ oats upon your perfume. . . . 
I drink the intoxication of the mingled scents
Of coconut oil, musk, and tar.14 
h e Vogue caption’s evocation of this poem—which was widely read in France and 
celebrated as an avatar of the modern literary tradition—would have resonated with a 
contemporary audience, tying Man Ray’s work to Baudelaire’s modern ideas.
In the photograph’s subsequent publication—in July 1928 in the Belgian journal 
Variétés and then in November in the Parisian monthly Art et Décoration—the title was 
pared down: mother-of-pearl became “white” and ebony became “black.” In the latter 
publication, a half-page reproduction of the image precedes an article by Pierre Migennes 
on the photographs of Man Ray. Discussing this picture before any other, the author 
noted with admiration: “h e same sleep and the same dream, the same mysterious marvel 
across time and space seem to unite these two female masks with closed eyes; one was 
hewn in the blackest of ebony by an African sculptor at a time unknown, the other, white 
but no less perfect, was made up yesterday in Paris.” h is text, unlike the one in Vogue, 
emphasized correspondence and similarities rather than diﬀ erence. Moreover, the notion 
of Kiki as having been “made up” encouraged the reading of the image as a fashion and 
fashionable photograph. h is was underscored by the caption, which explicitly identiﬁ ed 
the photograph as a composition for the magazine Vogue. Invoking the name of Vogue 
branded and clearly aﬀ ected perceptions of the photograph.15
From the outset, confusion existed about the title of the photograph. As of this 
writing, there is no evidence that Man Ray ever referred to the photograph by the title 
Noire et blanche. Vogue’s table of contents lists the work as Masque nègre (Negro Mask). 
In a letter to his sister Elsie, Man Ray refers to the photograph as “Kiki holding the 
African sculpture head.” On the verso of the print in the Baltimore Museum of Art, 
“Kiki with African Mask” is written in Man Ray’s hand; the National Gallery of Victoria 
also gives its print that title. Similarly, when the photograph began appearing at auction, 
it was usually listed as “Kiki and the African Mask” or some slight variation. h is was 
the case as well in the photograph’s early exhibition history. h e ﬁ rst known public 
display of the work was at h e First Comprehensive Exhibition of Foreign Commercial 
Photography, held at the Art Centre in New York in March 1931. In a full-page review of 
the exhibition in the Sunday rotogravure section of the New York Times, this photograph 
appeared as “Woman with a Mask.”16
h ree years later, in 1934, the image was included in an exhibition of Man Ray’s 
work organized by James h rall Soby at the Wadsworth Atheneum in Hartford, 
Connecticut, and in the Exposition Minotaure held at the Palais des Beaux-Arts in 
140    Summer 2006


## Page 221

141    American Art
Brussels. Recently discovered installation views of the comprehensive Hartford exhibi-
tion show Noire et blanche sharing a wall with portraits of Duchamp, Picasso, and poet 
Jean Cocteau, as well as a male torso and a study of rocks. While there is no indica-
tion that the photograph was given any speciﬁ c title at the Wadsworth Atheneum, it 
was displayed as Noire et blanche at the Minotaure exhibition.17 h e inclusion of the 
photograph in a retrospective exhibition devoted to the surrealist magazine Minotaure 
was ironic but telling, given that it never appeared in the magazine. Noire et blanche was 
virtually ignored by the vanguard publications. Nonetheless, by focusing on the photo-
graph’s dreamlike quality, disembodied forms, and incongruous juxtapositions—clearly 
borrowed from the surrealist ethos in which Man Ray was immersed—scholars over the 
past several decades have ahistorically repositioned it and claimed it as surrealism’s own.
h e highly reﬁ ned, polished, and stylized Baule-style mask featured in Noire et 
blanche appeared far more in tune, however, with a fashion aesthetic than with that 
of the surrealists, whose predilection for Oceanic art had superseded their interest in 
African art as an avenue to shock European sensibilities. By the 1920s the popular ap-
propriation of l’art nègre had, in the surrealists’ eyes, tamed African art. Even Cocteau 
had, by 1920, found the taste for African art so passé that he complained: “h e Negro 
craze has become as tiresome as the japonisme of Mallarmé’s generation.” While this 
statement is also indicative of Cocteau’s growing disenchantment with the avant-garde in 
the face of a postwar conservative backlash, it provides a barometer of shifting attitudes 
toward African art and culture. By contrast, the art of the South Seas, as Elizabeth 
Cowling notes, “repelled the formalists and had remained savage.” h is attitude was 
evident at the opening exhibition in 1926 of the Galerie Surréaliste, which took place 
two months before the ﬁ rst publication of Noire et blanche. h e exhibition featured 
Oceanic (but no African) works collected by the surrealists alongside work by Man Ray, 
presumably to suggest their aﬃ  nity. Moreover, since the notion of “convulsive beauty” 
of a transgressive nature proposed by André Breton was certainly not the type of beauty 
embodied in Noire et blanche, this photograph appears to have been little appreciated 
9 
First reproduction of Noire et 
blanche, in French Vogue, May 
1926, 36–37 © Vogue Paris 


## Page 222

within the surrealist inner circle. Its appeal to a fashion audience is reﬂ ected, however, 
in the fact that designer Jacques Doucet owned a print. While Doucet was sympathetic 
to surrealism and was an associate of Breton’s, he was a collector of both African and 
avant-garde art as well as being a leading fashion designer. His sensibilities arguably 
predisposed him to an appreciation of the intersection of creative impulses behind Man 
Ray’s photograph.18
h e retroactive positioning of Noire et blanche as a surrealist icon started soon after 
Man Ray’s death in 1976. Over the past three decades, the serious examination of surre-
alism (through the perspective of the New Art History) and the ﬂ ourishing photography 
market spurred interest in this image, conferring upon it a canonical status. Yet it is mis-
leading to situate the photograph as representative of that historical movement. Rather 
than making its mark within the surrealist milieu, this highly aestheticized composition 
juxtaposing the ultramodern woman, Kiki, with the purportedly ultra-“primitive” mask 
helped establish Man Ray’s reputation as a pioneer in the world of fashion photography. 
Sakier, on the other hand, virtually disappeared from the art-historical literature on this 
period, apart from brief mention in texts on industrial design or the decorative arts. 
His absence from discussion of Noire et blanche parallels the picture’s trajectory from 
fashion photograph to modernist icon; as the image became ever more closely tied to 
the surrealist narrative over the decades, its association with Vogue and the commercial 
designer Sakier was sidelined. Breton and the surrealists disdained and condemned the 
commercialization and trivializing of their cultural and political ideals manifest in the 
appropriation of surrealist style in popular venues such as fashion journals. While post-
modern scholarship takes a more sympathetic attitude toward the typically American 
mixing of “high” and “low” art, the commercial “taint” of Noire et blanche—in addition 
to playing into the popular vogue for African art—presumably made this image suspect 
within French surrealist circles at the time of its creation.
From Negative to Print
Just as the true identity and role of 
“Georges” Sakier in the making of 
Noire et blanche has until now eluded 
scholars, so have questions persisted 
about how the prints were made. h e 
controversy has been based in part 
on confusion about why the prints 
look the way they do. Each of the 
approximately twenty-four prints 
for which we can account at present 
varies in quality and appearance. h e 
paper textures and surfaces run from 
matte to glossy, from semitransparent 
to silver foiled, and from pebbled to 
a ﬁ ne, smooth surface. Some prints 
appear to have been generated from 
a dirty negative, while others are 
produced without a trace of dust or 
a scratch. Many are signed and dated 
in the negative, several are signed 
10 Man Ray, Noire et blanche (rever-
sal negative print), 1926 © 2006 
Man Ray Trust/Artists Rights 
Society (ARS), NY/ADAGP, Paris
142    Summer 2006


## Page 223

143    American Art
only on the mount, and at least one photograph has sig-
natures in both locations. A few are extensively retouched 
by hand, while others scarcely show the hand of the 
artist. In most, but not all, prints Man Ray has removed 
a distracting wisp of hair on Kiki’s left ear. Cropping is 
also inconsistent; in several of the prints the frame extends 
to include much of Kiki’s left arm and the tabletop while 
others are tightly cropped around her face and the mask. 
Further complicating matters, there are a small number of 
photographs horizontally ﬂ ipped and printed in the nega-
tive (ﬁ g. 10). Some examples were printed late in Man 
Ray’s life by a professional printer. An unknown number 
were printed posthumously. h e ongoing problem of 
missing negatives and internegatives, missing studio 
stamps, and fraudulently created photographs has only 
furthered uncertainty about this work.19
Since Man Ray said little about this photograph, 
period working negatives used in making early prints have 
been invaluable in understanding the creation of Noire 
et blanche as we know it today. As part of the settlement 
of the estate of his widow, Juliet Man Ray, the Man Ray 
Trust in 1994 donated to the Centre Georges Pompidou 
twelve thousand negatives, ﬁ ve thousand contact prints, 
and original art by the artist. Following an auction in 
1995 of other property, what was left of the Man Ray 
studio was moved to the trust oﬃ  ces in New York, where 
three elaborately retouched Noire et blanche negatives were 
belatedly discovered. Roughly half the known prints can 
be traced back to these three negatives. Over the past two years the working negatives, 
or internegatives, have been carefully analyzed and have undergone conservation. New 
contact prints have also been printed for study purposes.20
Each print Man Ray made represents a stage in the evolution of Noire et blanche. h e 
variety of techniques, papers, and cropping was the consequence of his protracted eﬀ ort to 
ﬁ nd what, for him at the time, was the perfect interpretation of the image. Noire et blanche 
did not exist fully formed in Man Ray’s original glass-plate negative—Kiki would undergo 
a miraculous facelift throughout the photograph’s evolution. From 1926 through 1933 the 
artist’s concept of a “deﬁ nitive image” was periodically reﬁ ned and revised. Indeed, it took 
Man Ray two years before he made the prints we today recognize as the standard print. 
By the time it appeared in Art et Décoration in November 1928, Man Ray had resolved 
innumerable problems with the image.
h e ﬁ rst photographs of 1926–27 were retouched in the actual print. Added highlights 
and contours emphasized the oval shape of Kiki’s head, creating a more perfect stream-
lined mate for the powerful mask. In the early prints, Man Ray used a larger portion of 
the negative. After these awkward ﬁ rst steps, he ﬁ gured out how he could manipulate 
the negative without danger to the original glass plate. Placing the glass-plate negative 
in an enlarger, Man Ray copied the image onto a large sheet of ﬁ lm measuring 9 ⁄ by 
11 ⁄ inches. h is second-generation negative is the ﬁ nal composition: compared with 
the original image in the glass-plate negative, the image on the ﬁ lm was cropped, re-
framed, and slighted softened. Every print that comes from this newly duplicated negative 
is a contact print. h e resulting negative was a positive image (ﬁ g. 11) resembling an 
11 Man Ray, Noire et blanche (large 
detail of retouched negative, 
for positive print), ca. 1926–28 
© 2006 Man Ray Trust/Artists 
Rights Society (ARS), NY/
ADAGP, Paris


## Page 224

oversize black-and-white transparency. Reversal, or negative, prints also were made from 
the ﬁ rst internegative. But before he could make the positive prints, Man Ray simply 
sandwiched another sheet of ﬁ lm with the previously made internegative (see ﬁ g. 1). Once 
exposed, processed, and dried, it provided a large surface for retouching.
Making copies of the original negative allowed Man Ray to preserve untouched 
the fragile glass plate. He labored over the negative with greater precision—ﬁ nessing, 
erasing, and reworking the smallest detail. Henceforth, any number of retouched pho-
tographs could be made. With a pencil and a ﬁ ne blade, the accomplished draftsman 
literally re-created his sitter. In the negative made for the positive print (see ﬁ g. 11), the 
eyelashes, eyebrows, nose, and mouth have been drawn in. h e shape of the face was 
gently modiﬁ ed. Highlights were added, while a mole and a wisp of errant hair over the 
ear were removed.
As with the countless patrons who sat for portraits, Man Ray’s retouching skills 
functioned to ﬂ atter the subject. In this instance, we see from unretouched prints that 
Kiki’s eyebrows were originally thicker, the lips less prominent. h e retouching, whether 
by hand on the print or in the negatives, has sought to correct perceived ﬂ aws in Kiki’s 
appearance (too much makeup and hair out of place) and to create a more harmonious 
visual mate for the mask (the completion of a more distinct and seamless oval shape and 
the elimination of visual distractions, such as softening details in the hair). It is worth 
noting that throughout the creative process Man Ray regarded the mask as perfect—for 
it remained untouched.
Few artists of the twentieth century were making photographs that looked like large 
paper negatives. Franz Roh, László Moholy-Nagy, and Man Ray in the twenties had 
been among the ﬁ rst. h e existence of a small number of negative prints of Noire et 
blanche (see ﬁ g. 10) has engendered a pressing debate. h e unprecedented oﬀ ering at 
auction in 1998 of a pair of positive and negative prints brought into the open questions 
about the authenticity of the negative (and horizontally ﬂ ipped) print. Experts and ama-
teurs argued whether the pair was ever made to be shown as a diptych.21 Fortunately, 
the internegatives prove that very early on Man Ray intended to make negative prints 
of Noire et blanche. h e two extant retouched negatives (the third being a copy) are each 
quite diﬀ erent. In the positive print, Man Ray altered but did not exaggerate the features 
of Kiki’s face. By contrast, the negative for making the reversed print does exaggerate 
some features so that lashes, eyes, lips, and nose stand out—against the dominant black 
face. h e eyelashes for the negative print are simpliﬁ ed; there are fewer individual hairs, 
so each one stands out more distinctly. In a ﬁ nely detailed naturalistic rendering they 
would be less legible. (Compare ﬁ g. 12, the “positive lash” with ﬁ g. 13, the “negative 
lash,” reversed here for comparison.) h e fully shaded lips appear white in the negative 
print, again to reinforce Kiki’s most general features. Clearly, the negative with the exag-
gerated retouching only makes sense as a negative print.22
Man Ray’s working process contradicts a persistent theory about Noire et blanche: 
conventional belief has been that he retouched a master print, then copied it to produce a 
new working negative that was used to create the subsequent prints.23 We now know this 
is not the case. h e process, in fact, began with enlarged copies of the original negative, 
which were retouched to make positive prints. All the retouching was done on this inter-
negative, with the exception of Man Ray’s signature and dating, which were etched on 
a subsequent internegative, presently unlocated. h e retouching on the negatives is per-
fectly visible in numerous prints and easily recognized. Unintended scratches in the nega-
tives are also apparent. At present, there are twelve known prints made from this process, 
and they are arguably the most accomplished of the Noire et blanche photographs. Within 
this group, Man Ray experimented with a range of photographic papers.
12 Man Ray, Noire et blanche (detail 
of retouched negative of left 
eyelashes, for positive print), 
ca. 1926–28 © 2006 Man Ray 
Trust/Artists Rights Society 
(ARS), NY/ADAGP, Paris
13 Man Ray, Noire et blanche (detail 
of retouched negative of left 
eyelashes, for negative print), 
ca. 1926–28 © 2006 Man Ray 
Trust/Artists Rights Society 
(ARS), NY/ADAGP, Paris
144    Summer 2006


## Page 225

145    American Art
In the thirties, Man Ray revisited Noire et blanche, 
creating his ﬁ nal and most dramatic interpretation. 
Abandoning his retouched negatives, Man Ray 
appears to have reverted to the original glass-plate 
negative to produce a new, tightly cropped version 
of the print. By the time he laid out the pages for his 
1934 monograph Man Ray Photographs, 1920–1934 
Paris, Noire et blanche had irrevocably changed. 
h is now classic volume includes the typical tight 
cropping of the later prints made until the time 
of his death. Kiki and the mask are uncomfort-
ably squeezed into the picture frame, while almost 
everything else is edited out. With the exception 
of the ﬁ ngers holding the mask, the hand and arm 
are absent, and the empty space used to balance the 
composition has been minimized. Later in his life, 
Man Ray frequently used this format for prints he 
made of Noire et blanche.
One remaining puzzle concerns the glass-plate 
negative for Noire et blanche in the Man Ray col-
lection at the Centre Georges Pompidou. Research 
conﬁ rms that it is a later copy, and is not the original, as previously assumed. In post-
humous reprints made from the original in the late 1970s, the table edge and tablecloth 
are visible (ﬁ g. 14). h is detail is absent in the Pompidou negative, leading to the con-
clusion that the original negative is missing.
Conclusion
h e story behind the making of Noire et blanche, as manifest here, is not simply black 
and white. If nothing else, the close reading of this iconic image underscores the 
contingency of photographic meaning and exposes how that meaning is constructed. 
h e intricate eighty-year history of its reception and the range of interpretations it 
has elicited reveal the work to be a multifaceted project of greater complexity than 
previously understood. Situating it within the context of Paris in the twenties and its 
publication as a fashionable image in Vogue magazine oﬀ ers new insight into its histori-
cal framework. Resolving the mystery of the mask’s source and thus introducing the 
contribution of George Sakier has added a new facet to the history of Noire et blanche, 
revealing a collaboration between two Americans in Paris whose creation left a lasting 
impact not only on fashion photography but also on the history of modernist photog-
raphy. Investigating the complex process that went into making the diﬀ erent print ver-
sions reveals much about Man Ray’s creative practice and answers persistent questions 
about the various prints and their afterlife. Indeed, it challenges the notion that the 
reproducible nature of the photographic medium renders all prints equal. Man Ray’s 
photographs are frequently unique, even as they exist in quantity. “Unmasking” the 
conception and execution of Noire et blanche demonstrates how even the most “iconic” 
images in the canon of modernism warrant scrutiny.

20 It was necessary for Man Ray to make 
two internegatives for the more well-
known positive Noire et blanche print. 
h e ﬁ rst working negative yielded a posi-
tive image. If printed as it was, it would 
produce a print resembling a negative; 
all the tones would be reversed. h ere-
fore, Man Ray had to sandwich this ﬁ rst 
internegative with a second unexposed 
sheet. Once exposed, the new resulting 
internegative would look similar to the 
original glass-plate negative. While the 
ﬁ rst internegative has all the retouching 
on it, the second internegative included 
the addition of the etched-in signature in 
block letters, along with the date 26. h e 
signed second internegative is unlocated. 
h e original internegatives, according to 
the Man Ray Trust, were recently discov-
ered in an old photo paper box; conser-
vation and testing of the internegatives 
was undertaken from fall 2004 through 
spring 2005 and new study prints from 
the internegatives were made.
